   <h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading">Halloween</h1> 
   <div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content"> 
    <div id="siteSub" class="noprint">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div> 
    <div id="contentSub"></div> 
    <div id="contentSub2"></div>

       Holiday celebrated on 31 October
      </div>

      <p><b>Halloween</b> or <b>Hallowe'en</b> (a contraction of "All Hallows' evening"),<sup id="cite_ref-Contraction_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Contraction-5">[5]</a></sup> also known as <b>Allhalloween</b>,<sup id="cite_ref-Palmer1882_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Palmer1882-6">[6]</a></sup> <b>All Hallows' Eve</b>,<sup id="cite_ref-Elwell2001_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Elwell2001-7">[7]</a></sup> or <b>All Saints' Eve</b>,<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> is a celebration <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Halloween" title="Geography of Halloween">observed in many countries</a> on 31 October , the eve of the <a href="/wiki/Western_Christian" class="mw-redirect" title="Western Christian">Western Christian</a> feast of <a href="/wiki/All_Saints%27_Day" title="All Saints' Day">All Hallows' Day</a>. It begins the observance of <a href="/wiki/Allhallowtide" title="Allhallowtide">Allhallowtide</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> the time in the <a href="/wiki/Liturgical_year" title="Liturgical year">liturgical year</a> dedicated to remembering the dead, including <a href="/wiki/Saint" title="Saint">saints</a> (hallows), <a href="/wiki/Christian_martyr" title="Christian martyr">martyrs</a>, and all the faithful departed.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Davis2009_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davis2009-11">[11]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>One theory holds that many Halloween traditions may have been influenced by ancient <a href="/wiki/Celts" title="Celts">Celtic</a> <a href="/wiki/Harvest_festival" title="Harvest festival">harvest festivals</a>, particularly the <a href="/wiki/Gaels" title="Gaels">Gaelic</a> festival <a href="/wiki/Samhain" title="Samhain">Samhain</a>, which may have had <a href="/wiki/Paganism" title="Paganism">pagan</a> roots;<sup id="cite_ref-Smith2004_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smith2004-12">[12]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Christianity1_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Christianity1-13">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Christianity2_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Christianity2-14">[14]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Christianity3_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Christianity3-15">[15]</a></sup> some scholars hold that Samhain may have been <a href="/wiki/Christianization" title="Christianization">Christianized</a> as <a href="/wiki/All_Hallow%27s_Day" class="mw-redirect" title="All Hallow's Day">All Hallow's Day</a>, along with its eve, by the <a href="/wiki/Early_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Early Church">early Church</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Roberts1987_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Roberts1987-16">[16]</a></sup> Other academics believe, however, that Halloween began solely as a <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christian</a> holiday, being the <a href="/wiki/Vigil#Eves_of_religious_celebrations" title="Vigil">vigil</a> of All Hallow's Day.<sup id="cite_ref-FoleyO’Donnell2008_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FoleyO’Donnell2008-17">[17]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Barr2016_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barr2016-18">[18]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Halloween activities include <a href="/wiki/Trick-or-treating" title="Trick-or-treating">trick-or-treating</a> (or the related <a href="/wiki/Guising" class="mw-redirect" title="Guising">guising</a> and <a href="/wiki/Souling" class="mw-redirect" title="Souling">souling</a>), attending <a href="/wiki/Halloween_costume" title="Halloween costume">Halloween costume</a> parties, carving pumpkins into <a href="/wiki/Jack-o%27-lantern" title="Jack-o'-lantern">jack-o'-lanterns</a>, lighting <a href="/wiki/Bonfire" title="Bonfire">bonfires</a>, <a href="/wiki/Apple_bobbing" title="Apple bobbing">apple bobbing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fortune-telling" title="Fortune-telling">divination</a> games, playing <a href="/wiki/Prank" class="mw-redirect" title="Prank">pranks</a>, visiting <a href="/wiki/Haunted_attraction_(simulated)" title="Haunted attraction (simulated)">haunted attractions</a>, telling scary stories, as well as watching <a href="/wiki/List_of_films_set_around_Halloween" title="List of films set around Halloween">horror films</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Fieldhouse2017p256_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fieldhouse2017p256-21">[21]</a></sup> In many parts of the world, the Christian religious observances of All Hallows' Eve, including attending church services and lighting <a href="/wiki/Votive_candle" title="Votive candle">candles</a> on the graves of the dead, remain popular,<sup id="cite_ref-Skog2008_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Skog2008-22">[22]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> although elsewhere it is a more commercial and secular celebration.<sup id="cite_ref-Hynes1993_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hynes1993-25">[25]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BradenVillage1988_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BradenVillage1988-27">[27]</a></sup> Some Christians historically <a href="/wiki/Meat-free_day" class="mw-redirect" title="Meat-free day">abstained from meat</a> on All Hallows' Eve, a tradition reflected in the eating of certain <a href="/wiki/Vegetarian" class="mw-redirect" title="Vegetarian">vegetarian</a> foods on this <a href="/wiki/Vigil" title="Vigil">vigil</a> day, including apples, <a href="/wiki/Potato_pancake" title="Potato pancake">potato pancakes</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Soul_cake" title="Soul cake">soul cakes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mahon1991_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mahon1991-30">[30]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Fieldhouse2017_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fieldhouse2017-31">[31]</a></sup> </p>

        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-23"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">15</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li> 
       </ul> 
      </div> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:J._M._Wright_-_Edward_Scriven_-_Robert_Burns_-_Halloween.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/J._M._Wright_-_Edward_Scriven_-_Robert_Burns_-_Halloween.JPG/220px-J._M._Wright_-_Edward_Scriven_-_Robert_Burns_-_Halloween.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="189" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/J._M._Wright_-_Edward_Scriven_-_Robert_Burns_-_Halloween.JPG/330px-J._M._Wright_-_Edward_Scriven_-_Robert_Burns_-_Halloween.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/J._M._Wright_-_Edward_Scriven_-_Robert_Burns_-_Halloween.JPG/440px-J._M._Wright_-_Edward_Scriven_-_Robert_Burns_-_Halloween.JPG 2x" data-file-width="4568" data-file-height="3928"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:J._M._Wright_-_Edward_Scriven_-_Robert_Burns_-_Halloween.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The word appears as the title of <a href="/wiki/Robert_Burns" title="Robert Burns">Robert Burns</a>' "<a href="/wiki/Halloween_(poem)" title="Halloween (poem)">Halloween</a>" (1785), a poem traditionally recited by Scots.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The word <i>Halloween</i> or <i>Hallowe'en</i> dates to about 1745<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> and is of Christian origin.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> The word "Hallowe'en" means "<a href="/wiki/Saint" title="Saint">Saints'</a> evening".<sup id="cite_ref-Luck1998_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Luck1998-34">[34]</a></sup> It comes from a Scottish term for <i>All Hallows' Eve</i> (the evening before <a href="/wiki/All_Saints%27_Day" title="All Saints' Day">All Hallows' Day</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-oed_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oed-35">[35]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Scots_language" title="Scots language">Scots</a>, the word "eve" is <i>even</i>, and this is contracted to <i>e'en</i> or <i>een</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> Over time, <i>(All) Hallow(s) E(v)en</i> evolved into <i>Hallowe'en</i>. Although the phrase "All Hallows'" is found in <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a>, "All Hallows' Eve" is itself not seen until 1556.<sup id="cite_ref-oed_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oed-35">[35]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-DSL_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DSL-37">[37]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gaelic_influence">Gaelic influence</span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Traditional-irish-halloween-mask.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Traditional-irish-halloween-mask.jpg/200px-Traditional-irish-halloween-mask.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="110" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Traditional-irish-halloween-mask.jpg/300px-Traditional-irish-halloween-mask.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Traditional-irish-halloween-mask.jpg/400px-Traditional-irish-halloween-mask.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="552"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Traditional-irish-halloween-mask.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>An early 20th-century Irish Halloween mask displayed at the <a href="/wiki/Museum_of_Country_Life" class="mw-redirect" title="Museum of Country Life">Museum of Country Life</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Today's Halloween customs are thought to have been influenced by <a href="/wiki/Folk_culture" class="mw-redirect" title="Folk culture">folk</a> customs and beliefs from the <a href="/wiki/Celtic_nations" title="Celtic nations">Celtic-speaking countries</a>, some of which are believed to have <a href="/wiki/Celtic_polytheism" class="mw-redirect" title="Celtic polytheism">pagan</a> roots.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jack_Santino" title="Jack Santino">Jack Santino</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Folkloristics" class="mw-redirect" title="Folkloristics">folklorist</a>, writes that "there was throughout Ireland an uneasy truce existing between customs and beliefs associated with Christianity and those associated with religions that were Irish before Christianity arrived".<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> Historian Nicholas Rogers, exploring the origins of Halloween, notes that while "some folklorists have detected its origins in the Roman feast of <a href="/wiki/Pomona_(mythology)" title="Pomona (mythology)">Pomona</a>, the goddess of fruits and seeds, or in the <a href="/wiki/Festival_of_the_dead" class="mw-redirect" title="Festival of the dead">festival of the dead</a> called <a href="/wiki/Parentalia" title="Parentalia">Parentalia</a>, it is more typically linked to the Celtic festival of <a href="/wiki/Samhain" title="Samhain">Samhain</a>, which comes from the <a href="/wiki/Old_Irish" title="Old Irish">Old Irish</a> for 'summer's end'."<sup id="cite_ref-rogers_s_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rogers_s-40">[40]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Samhain (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="/ɑː/: 'a' in 'father'">ɑː</span><span title="'w' in 'wind'">w</span><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span></span>,<span class="wrap"> </span><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="/aʊ/: 'ou' in 'mouth'">aʊ</span><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span></span>/</a></span></span>) was the first and most important of the four <a href="/wiki/Quarter_days" title="Quarter days">quarter days</a> in the medieval <a href="/wiki/Gaels" title="Gaels">Gaelic</a> calendar and was celebrated on 31 October – 1 November<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> in Ireland, <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Man" title="Isle of Man">Isle of Man</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Roud_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Roud-42">[42]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-bbc_halloween_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc_halloween-43">[43]</a></sup> A kindred festival was held at the same time of year by the <a href="/wiki/Brittonic_languages" title="Brittonic languages">Brittonic</a> Celts, called <a href="/wiki/Calan_Gaeaf" title="Calan Gaeaf">Calan Gaeaf</a> in Wales, <a href="/wiki/Allantide" title="Allantide">Kalan Gwav</a> in Cornwall and Kalan Goañv in <a href="/wiki/Brittany" title="Brittany">Brittany</a>; a name meaning "first day of winter". For the Celts, the day ended and began at sunset; thus the festival began on the evening before 7 November by modern reckoning (the half point between equinox and solstice).<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> Samhain is mentioned in some of the earliest Irish literature. The names have been used by historians to refer to Celtic Halloween customs up until the 19th century,<sup id="cite_ref-hutton365-369_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hutton365-369-45">[45]</a></sup> and are still the Gaelic and Welsh names for Halloween. </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Snap-Apple_Night_globalphilosophy.PNG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Snap-Apple_Night_globalphilosophy.PNG/230px-Snap-Apple_Night_globalphilosophy.PNG" decoding="async" width="230" height="155" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Snap-Apple_Night_globalphilosophy.PNG/345px-Snap-Apple_Night_globalphilosophy.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Snap-Apple_Night_globalphilosophy.PNG/460px-Snap-Apple_Night_globalphilosophy.PNG 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="539"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Snap-Apple_Night_globalphilosophy.PNG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><i>Snap-Apple Night</i>, painted by <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Maclise" title="Daniel Maclise">Daniel Maclise</a> in 1833, shows people feasting and playing divination games on Halloween in Ireland.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Samhain marked the end of the <a href="/wiki/Harvest" title="Harvest">harvest</a> season and the beginning of winter or the 'darker half' of the year.<sup id="cite_ref-monaghan407_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-monaghan407-46">[46]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> Like <a href="/wiki/Beltane" title="Beltane">Beltane</a>/<a href="/wiki/Calan_Mai" title="Calan Mai">Calan Mai</a>, it was seen as a <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/liminal" class="extiw" title="wikt:liminal">liminal</a> time, when the boundary between this world and the <a href="/wiki/Celtic_Otherworld" title="Celtic Otherworld">Otherworld</a> thinned. This meant the <i><a href="/wiki/Aos_S%C3%AD" title="Aos Sí">Aos Sí</a></i> (Connacht pronunciation <span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/iː/: 'ee' in 'fleece'">iː</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="/ʃ/: 'sh' in 'shy'">ʃ</span><span title="/iː/: 'ee' in 'fleece'">iː</span></span>/</a></span></span> <a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling">eess-<span style="font-size:90%">SHEE</span></i></a>, Munster /e:s ʃi:/), the '<a href="/wiki/Spirit" title="Spirit">spirits</a>' or '<a href="/wiki/Fairy" title="Fairy">fairies</a>', could more easily come into this world and were particularly active.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> Most scholars see the <i>Aos Sí</i> as "degraded versions of ancient gods [...] whose power remained active in the people's minds even after they had been officially replaced by later religious beliefs".<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> The <i>Aos Sí</i> were both respected and feared, with individuals often invoking the protection of <a href="/wiki/God_in_Christianity" title="God in Christianity">God</a> when approaching their dwellings.<sup id="cite_ref-Monaghan2009_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Monaghan2009-51">[51]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> At Samhain, it was believed that the <i>Aos Sí</i> needed to be <a href="/wiki/Propitiation" title="Propitiation">propitiated</a> to ensure that the people and their livestock survived the winter. Offerings of food and drink, or portions of the crops, were left outside for the <i>Aos Sí</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> The souls of the dead were also said to revisit their homes seeking <a href="/wiki/Hospitality" title="Hospitality">hospitality</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> Places were set at the dinner table and by the fire to welcome them.<sup id="cite_ref-mcneill1_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mcneill1-57">[57]</a></sup> The belief that the souls of the dead return home on one night of the year and must be appeased seems to have ancient origins and is found in many cultures throughout the world.<sup id="cite_ref-miles_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-miles-58">[58]</a></sup> In 19th century Ireland, "candles would be lit and <a href="/wiki/Christian_prayer" title="Christian prayer">prayers</a> formally offered for the souls of the dead. After this the eating, drinking, and games would begin".<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Throughout Ireland and Britain, the household festivities included rituals and games intended to foretell one's future, especially regarding death and marriage.<sup id="cite_ref-Hutton,_p.380_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hutton,_p.380-60">[60]</a></sup> Apples and nuts were often used in these <a href="/wiki/Divination" title="Divination">divination</a> rituals. They included <a href="/wiki/Apple_bobbing" title="Apple bobbing">apple bobbing</a>, nut roasting, <a href="/wiki/Scrying" title="Scrying">scrying</a> or mirror-gazing, <a href="/wiki/Molybdomancy" title="Molybdomancy">pouring molten lead</a> or <a href="/wiki/Oomancy" title="Oomancy">egg whites</a> into water, <a href="/wiki/Dream_interpretation" title="Dream interpretation">dream interpretation</a>, and others.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> Special <a href="/wiki/Bonfire" title="Bonfire">bonfires</a> were lit and there were rituals involving them. Their flames, smoke and ashes were deemed to have protective and cleansing powers, and were also used for divination.<sup id="cite_ref-monaghan407_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-monaghan407-46">[46]</a></sup> In some places, torches lit from the bonfire were carried <a href="/wiki/Sunwise" title="Sunwise">sunwise</a> around homes and fields to protect them.<sup id="cite_ref-hutton365-369_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hutton365-369-45">[45]</a></sup> It is suggested that the fires were a kind of <a href="/wiki/Sympathetic_magic" title="Sympathetic magic">imitative or sympathetic magic</a> – they mimicked the Sun, helping the "powers of growth" and holding back the decay and darkness of winter.<sup id="cite_ref-mcneill1_57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mcneill1-57">[57]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-frazer63_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-frazer63-62">[62]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-macculloch_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-macculloch-63">[63]</a></sup> In Scotland, these bonfires and divination games were banned by the church elders in some parishes.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> In Wales, bonfires were lit to "prevent the souls of the dead from falling to earth".<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> Later, these bonfires served to keep "away the <a href="/wiki/Devil_in_Christianity" title="Devil in Christianity">devil</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-Rosinsky2002_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rosinsky2002-66">[66]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Traditional_Irish_halloween_Jack-o%27-lantern.jpg" class="image"><img alt="photograph" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Traditional_Irish_halloween_Jack-o%27-lantern.jpg/150px-Traditional_Irish_halloween_Jack-o%27-lantern.jpg" decoding="async" width="150" height="193" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Traditional_Irish_halloween_Jack-o%27-lantern.jpg/225px-Traditional_Irish_halloween_Jack-o%27-lantern.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Traditional_Irish_halloween_Jack-o%27-lantern.jpg/300px-Traditional_Irish_halloween_Jack-o%27-lantern.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="1287"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Traditional_Irish_halloween_Jack-o%27-lantern.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>A plaster cast of a traditional Irish Halloween turnip (rutabaga) lantern on display in the <a href="/wiki/Museum_of_Country_Life" class="mw-redirect" title="Museum of Country Life">Museum of Country Life</a>, Ireland<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>From at least the 16th century,<sup id="cite_ref-mcneill2_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mcneill2-68">[68]</a></sup> the festival included <a href="/wiki/Mummers_Play" class="mw-redirect" title="Mummers Play">mumming</a> and <a href="/wiki/Guising" class="mw-redirect" title="Guising">guising</a> in Ireland, Scotland, the Isle of Man and Wales.<sup id="cite_ref-hutton379-383_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hutton379-383-69">[69]</a></sup> This involved people going house-to-house in costume (or in disguise), usually reciting verses or songs in exchange for food. It may have originally been a tradition whereby people impersonated the <i>Aos Sí</i>, or the souls of the dead, and received offerings on their behalf, similar to the custom of <a href="/wiki/Soul_cake" title="Soul cake">souling</a> (see <a href="/wiki/Halloween#Christian_influence" title="Halloween">below</a>). Impersonating these beings, or wearing a disguise, was also believed to protect oneself from them.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> It is suggested that the mummers and guisers "personify the old spirits of the winter, who demanded reward in exchange for good fortune".<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> In parts of southern Ireland, the guisers included a <a href="/wiki/Hobby_horse" title="Hobby horse">hobby horse</a>. A man dressed as a <i>Láir Bhán</i> (<a href="/wiki/White_horse_(mythology)" class="mw-redirect" title="White horse (mythology)">white mare</a>) led youths house-to-house reciting verses&nbsp;– some of which had pagan overtones&nbsp;– in exchange for food. If the household donated food it could expect good fortune from the 'Muck Olla'; not doing so would bring misfortune.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> In Scotland, youths went house-to-house with masked, painted or blackened faces, often threatening to do mischief if they were not welcomed.<sup id="cite_ref-hutton379-383_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hutton379-383-69">[69]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/F._Marian_McNeill" title="F. Marian McNeill">F. Marian McNeill</a> suggests the ancient festival included people in costume representing the spirits, and that faces were marked (or blackened) with ashes taken from the sacred bonfire.<sup id="cite_ref-mcneill2_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mcneill2-68">[68]</a></sup> In parts of Wales, men went about dressed as fearsome beings called <i>gwrachod</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-hutton379-383_69-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hutton379-383-69">[69]</a></sup> In the late 19th and early 20th century, young people in <a href="/wiki/Glamorgan" title="Glamorgan">Glamorgan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Orkney" title="Orkney">Orkney</a> <a href="/wiki/Cross-dressing" title="Cross-dressing">cross-dressed</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-hutton379-383_69-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hutton379-383-69">[69]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Elsewhere in Europe, mumming and hobby horses were part of other yearly festivals. However, in the Celtic-speaking regions, they were "particularly appropriate to a night upon which supernatural beings were said to be abroad and could be imitated or warded off by human wanderers".<sup id="cite_ref-hutton379-383_69-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hutton379-383-69">[69]</a></sup> From at least the 18th century, "imitating malignant spirits" led to playing pranks in Ireland and the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Highlands" title="Scottish Highlands">Scottish Highlands</a>. Wearing costumes and playing pranks at Halloween spread to England in the 20th century.<sup id="cite_ref-hutton379-383_69-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hutton379-383-69">[69]</a></sup> Traditionally, pranksters used hollowed out <a href="/wiki/Rutabaga" title="Rutabaga">turnips</a> or <a href="/wiki/Mangelwurzel" title="Mangelwurzel">mangel wurzels</a> often carved with grotesque faces as lanterns.<sup id="cite_ref-hutton379-383_69-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hutton379-383-69">[69]</a></sup> By those who made them, the lanterns were variously said to represent the spirits,<sup id="cite_ref-hutton379-383_69-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hutton379-383-69">[69]</a></sup> or were <a href="/wiki/Apotropaic_magic" title="Apotropaic magic">used to ward off</a> evil spirits.<sup id="cite_ref-palmer87_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-palmer87-73">[73]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> They were common in parts of Ireland and the Scottish Highlands in the 19th century,<sup id="cite_ref-hutton379-383_69-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hutton379-383-69">[69]</a></sup> as well as in <a href="/wiki/Somerset" title="Somerset">Somerset</a> (see <a href="/wiki/Punkie_Night" title="Punkie Night">Punkie Night</a>). In the 20th century they spread to other parts of England and became generally known as <a href="/wiki/Jack-o%27-lantern" title="Jack-o'-lantern">jack-o'-lanterns</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-hutton379-383_69-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hutton379-383-69">[69]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Christian_influence">Christian influence</span></h3> 
      <p>Today's Halloween customs are thought to have been influenced by Christian dogma and practices derived from it.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> Halloween is the evening before the Christian holy days of <a href="/wiki/All_Saints%27_Day" title="All Saints' Day">All Hallows' Day</a> (also known as <i>All Saints' or Hallowmas</i>) on 1 November and <a href="/wiki/All_Souls%27_Day" title="All Souls' Day">All Souls' Day</a> on 2 November, thus giving the holiday on 31 October the full name of <i>All Hallows' Eve</i> (meaning the evening before All Hallows' Day).<sup id="cite_ref-rogers_religion_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rogers_religion-76">[76]</a></sup> Since the time of the <a href="/wiki/Early_Christianity" class="mw-redirect" title="Early Christianity">early Church</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Calendar_of_saints#Ranking_of_feast_days" title="Calendar of saints">major feasts</a> in Christianity (such as Christmas, Easter and <a href="/wiki/Pentecost" title="Pentecost">Pentecost</a>) had <a href="/wiki/Vigil#Eves_of_religious_celebrations" title="Vigil">vigils</a> that began the night before, as did the feast of All Hallows'.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> These three days are collectively called <a href="/wiki/Allhallowtide" title="Allhallowtide">Allhallowtide</a> and are a time for honoring the <a href="/wiki/Saint" title="Saint">saints</a> and praying for the recently departed <a href="/wiki/Soul_in_the_Bible" title="Soul in the Bible">souls</a> who have yet to reach Heaven. Commemorations of all saints and martyrs were held by several churches on various dates, mostly in springtime.<sup id="cite_ref-CP_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CP-79">[79]</a></sup> In 609, <a href="/wiki/Pope_Boniface_IV" title="Pope Boniface IV">Pope Boniface IV</a> <a href="/wiki/Christianized_sites" title="Christianized sites">re-dedicated</a> the <a href="/wiki/Pantheon,_Rome" title="Pantheon, Rome">Pantheon</a> in Rome to "St Mary and all martyrs" on 13 May. This was the same date as <a href="/wiki/Lemuria_(festival)" title="Lemuria (festival)">Lemuria</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_ancient_Rome" title="Religion in ancient Rome">ancient Roman</a> festival of the dead, and the same date as the commemoration of all saints in <a href="/wiki/Edessa" title="Edessa">Edessa</a> in the time of <a href="/wiki/Ephrem_the_Syrian" title="Ephrem the Syrian">Ephrem the Syrian</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Saunders_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Saunders-80">[80]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The feast of All Hallows', on its current date in the Western Church, may be traced to <a href="/wiki/Pope_Gregory_III" title="Pope Gregory III">Pope Gregory III</a>'s (731–741) founding of an <a href="/wiki/Oratory_(worship)" title="Oratory (worship)">oratory</a> in <a href="/wiki/Old_St._Peter%27s_Basilica" title="Old St. Peter's Basilica">St Peter's</a> for the relics "of the holy apostles and of all saints, martyrs and confessors".<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> In 835, All Hallows' Day was officially switched to 1 November, the same date as Samhain, at the behest of <a href="/wiki/Pope_Gregory_IV" title="Pope Gregory IV">Pope Gregory IV</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-hutton364_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hutton364-83">[83]</a></sup> Some suggest this was due to Celtic influence, while others suggest it was a Germanic idea,<sup id="cite_ref-hutton364_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hutton364-83">[83]</a></sup> although it is claimed that both Germanic and Celtic-speaking peoples commemorated the dead at the beginning of winter.<sup id="cite_ref-macculloch10_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-macculloch10-84">[84]</a></sup> They may have seen it as the most fitting time to do so, as it is a time of 'dying' in nature.<sup id="cite_ref-hutton364_83-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hutton364-83">[83]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-macculloch10_84-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-macculloch10-84">[84]</a></sup> It is also suggested that the change was made on the "practical grounds that Rome in summer could not accommodate the great number of pilgrims who flocked to it", and perhaps because of <a href="/wiki/Public_health" title="Public health">public health</a> considerations regarding <a href="/wiki/Roman_Fever_(disease)" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Fever (disease)">Roman Fever</a> – a disease that claimed a number of lives during the sultry summers of the region.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> </p> 
      <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r978413945/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{display:flex;flex-direction:row;clear:left;flex-wrap:wrap;width:100%;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{margin:1px;float:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .theader{clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;align-self:center;background-color:transparent;width:100%}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-left{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-right{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-center{text-align:center}@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;max-width:none!important;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{justify-content:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle .thumbcaption{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow>.thumbcaption{text-align:center}}</style>
      <div class="thumb tmulti tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:254px;max-width:254px">
        <div class="trow">
         <div class="tsingle" style="width:252px;max-width:252px">
          <div class="thumbimage">
           <a href="/wiki/File:Halloween_Bangladesh.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Halloween_Bangladesh.jpg/250px-Halloween_Bangladesh.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="167" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Halloween_Bangladesh.jpg/375px-Halloween_Bangladesh.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Halloween_Bangladesh.jpg/500px-Halloween_Bangladesh.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4368" data-file-height="2912"></a>
          </div>
         </div>
        </div>
        <div class="trow">
         <div class="tsingle" style="width:252px;max-width:252px">
          <div class="thumbimage">
           <a href="/wiki/File:Halloween_Sweden.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Halloween_Sweden.png/250px-Halloween_Sweden.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="206" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Halloween_Sweden.png/375px-Halloween_Sweden.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Halloween_Sweden.png/500px-Halloween_Sweden.png 2x" data-file-width="625" data-file-height="514"></a>
          </div>
         </div>
        </div>
        <div class="trow" style="display:flex">
         <div class="thumbcaption">
          On All Hallows' Eve, Christians in some parts of the world visit cemeteries to pray and place flowers and candles on the graves of their loved ones.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> The top photograph shows <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_Bangladesh" title="Christianity in Bangladesh">Bangladeshi Christians</a> lighting candles on the headstone of a relative, while the bottom photograph shows <a href="/wiki/Lutheran" class="mw-redirect" title="Lutheran">Lutheran Christians</a> praying and lighting candles in front of the central crucifix of a graveyard.
         </div>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>By the end of the 12th century they had become <a href="/wiki/Holy_day_of_obligation" title="Holy day of obligation">holy days of obligation</a> across Europe and involved such traditions as ringing <a href="/wiki/Church_bell" title="Church bell">church bells</a> for the souls in <a href="/wiki/Purgatory" title="Purgatory">purgatory</a>. In addition, "it was customary for <a href="/wiki/Town_crier" title="Town crier">criers</a> dressed in black to parade the streets, ringing a bell of mournful sound and calling on all good Christians to remember the poor souls."<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> "Souling", the custom of baking and sharing <a href="/wiki/Soul_cake" title="Soul cake">soul cakes</a> for all <a href="/wiki/Baptism" title="Baptism">christened</a> souls,<sup id="cite_ref-AFP_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AFP-88">[88]</a></sup> has been suggested as the origin of trick-or-treating.<sup id="cite_ref-Britannica_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-89">[89]</a></sup> The custom dates back at least as far as the 15th century<sup id="cite_ref-hutton374-375_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hutton374-375-90">[90]</a></sup> and was found in parts of England, Flanders, Germany and Austria.<sup id="cite_ref-miles_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-miles-58">[58]</a></sup> Groups of poor people, often children, would go door-to-door during Allhallowtide, collecting soul cakes, in exchange for <a href="/wiki/Prayer_for_the_dead" title="Prayer for the dead">praying for the dead</a>, especially the souls of the givers' friends and relatives.<sup id="cite_ref-hutton374-375_90-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hutton374-375-90">[90]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Dodge_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dodge-91">[91]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-DeMello2012_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DeMello2012-92">[92]</a></sup> Soul cakes would also be offered for the souls themselves to eat,<sup id="cite_ref-miles_58-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-miles-58">[58]</a></sup> or the 'soulers' would act as their representatives.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> As with the <a href="/wiki/Lent" title="Lent">Lenten</a> tradition of <a href="/wiki/Hot_cross_bun" title="Hot cross bun">hot cross buns</a>, Allhallowtide soul cakes were often marked with a <a href="/wiki/Christian_cross" title="Christian cross">cross</a>, indicating that they were baked as <a href="/wiki/Alms#Christianity" title="Alms">alms</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Levene2016_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Levene2016-94">[94]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Shakespeare" class="mw-redirect" title="Shakespeare">Shakespeare</a> mentions souling in his comedy <i><a href="/wiki/The_Two_Gentlemen_of_Verona" title="The Two Gentlemen of Verona">The Two Gentlemen of Verona</a></i> (1593).<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> On the custom of wearing costumes, Christian minister Prince Sorie Conteh wrote: "It was traditionally believed that the souls of the departed wandered the earth until All Saints' Day, and All Hallows' Eve provided one last chance for the dead to gain vengeance on their enemies before moving to the <a href="/wiki/Christian_eschatology" title="Christian eschatology">next world</a>. In order to avoid being recognized by any soul that might be seeking such vengeance, people would don masks or costumes to disguise their identities".<sup id="cite_ref-Christian_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Christian-96">[96]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>It is claimed that in the Middle Ages, churches that were too poor to display the <a href="/wiki/Relic" title="Relic">relics</a> of <a href="/wiki/Christian_martyr" title="Christian martyr">martyred saints</a> at Allhallowtide let parishioners dress up as saints instead.<sup id="cite_ref-Bannatyne1998_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bannatyne1998-97">[97]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Morrow2001_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Morrow2001-98">[98]</a></sup> Some Christians continue to observe this custom at Halloween today.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Lesley_Bannatyne" title="Lesley Bannatyne">Lesley Bannatyne</a> believes this could have been a Christianization of an earlier pagan custom.<sup id="cite_ref-bannatyne9_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bannatyne9-100">[100]</a></sup> While souling, Christians would carry with them "lanterns made of hollowed-out turnips".<sup id="cite_ref-PulliamFonseca2016_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PulliamFonseca2016-101">[101]</a></sup> It has been suggested that the carved jack-o'-lantern, a popular symbol of Halloween, originally represented the souls of the dead.<sup id="cite_ref-rogers57_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rogers57-102">[102]</a></sup> On Halloween, in medieval Europe, fires served a dual purpose, being lit to guide returning souls to the homes of their families, as well as to deflect demons from haunting sincere Christian folk.<sup id="cite_ref-CarterPetro1998_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CarterPetro1998-103">[103]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> Households in Austria, England and Ireland often had "candles burning in every room to guide the souls back to visit their earthly homes". These were known as "soul lights".<sup id="cite_ref-Think_page_15_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Think_page_15-105">[105]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Santino,_p.95_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Santino,_p.95-106">[106]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> Many Christians in mainland Europe, especially in France, believed "that once a year, on Hallowe'en, the dead of the churchyards rose for one wild, hideous carnival" known as the <i><a href="/wiki/Danse_macabre" class="mw-redirect" title="Danse macabre">danse macabre</a></i>, which has often been depicted in <a href="/wiki/Christian_art" title="Christian art">church decoration</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Allmand" title="Christopher Allmand">Christopher Allmand</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rosamond_McKitterick" title="Rosamond McKitterick">Rosamond McKitterick</a> write in <i>The New Cambridge Medieval History</i> that "Christians were moved by the sight of the <a href="/wiki/Infant_Jesus" class="mw-redirect" title="Infant Jesus">Infant Jesus</a> playing on his mother's knee; their hearts were touched by the <a href="/wiki/Piet%C3%A0" title="Pietà">Pietà</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Patron_saint" title="Patron saint">patron saints</a> reassured them by their presence. But, all the while, the <i>danse macabre</i> urged them not to forget the end of all earthly things."<sup id="cite_ref-Allmand1998_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Allmand1998-109">[109]</a></sup> This <i>danse macabre</i> was enacted at village pageants and at <a href="/wiki/Masque" title="Masque">court masques</a>, with people "dressing up as corpses from various strata of society", and may have been the origin of modern-day Halloween costume parties.<sup id="cite_ref-PulliamFonseca2016_101-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PulliamFonseca2016-101">[101]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hörandner2005_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hörandner2005-111">[111]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In parts of Britain, these customs came under attack during the <a href="/wiki/English_Reformation" title="English Reformation">Reformation</a> as some <a href="/wiki/Protestant" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant">Protestants</a> berated purgatory as a "<a href="/wiki/Popish" class="mw-redirect" title="Popish">popish</a>" doctrine incompatible with their notion of <a href="/wiki/Predestination" title="Predestination">predestination</a>. Thus, for some <a href="/wiki/Nonconformist_(Protestantism)" title="Nonconformist (Protestantism)">Nonconformist Protestants</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Christian_theology" title="Christian theology">theology</a> of All Hallows' Eve was redefined; without the doctrine of purgatory, "the returning souls cannot be journeying from Purgatory on their way to Heaven, as Catholics frequently believe and assert. Instead, the so-called ghosts are thought to be in actuality evil spirits. As such they are threatening."<sup id="cite_ref-Santino,_p.95_106-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Santino,_p.95-106">[106]</a></sup> Other Protestants maintained belief in an <a href="/wiki/Intermediate_state" title="Intermediate state">intermediate state</a>, known as <a href="/wiki/Christian_views_on_Hades" title="Christian views on Hades">Hades</a> (<a href="/wiki/Bosom_of_Abraham" title="Bosom of Abraham">Bosom of Abraham</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> and continued to observe the original customs, especially souling, <a href="/wiki/Votive_candle" title="Votive candle">candlelit</a> <a href="/wiki/Procession" title="Procession">processions</a> and the ringing of church bells in memory of the dead.<sup id="cite_ref-rogers_religion_76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rogers_religion-76">[76]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mosteller_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mosteller-113">[113]</a></sup> Mark Donnelly, a professor of <a href="/wiki/Medieval_archaeology" title="Medieval archaeology">medieval archaeology</a>, and historian Daniel Diehl, with regard to the <a href="/wiki/Unclean_spirit" title="Unclean spirit">evil spirits</a>, on Halloween, write that "barns and <a href="/wiki/House_blessing" title="House blessing">homes were blessed</a> to protect people and livestock from the <a href="/wiki/Christian_views_on_magic" title="Christian views on magic">effect of witches</a>, who were believed to accompany the malignant spirits as they traveled the earth."<sup id="cite_ref-Medieval_Celebrations_page_17_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Medieval_Celebrations_page_17-114">[114]</a></sup> In the 19th century, in some rural parts of England, families gathered on hills on the night of All Hallows' Eve. One held a bunch of burning straw on a <a href="/wiki/Pitchfork" title="Pitchfork">pitchfork</a> while the rest knelt around him in a circle, praying for the souls of relatives and friends until the flames went out. This was known as <i>teen'lay</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Hutton2001_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hutton2001-115">[115]</a></sup> Other customs included the tindle fires in Derbyshire and all-night vigil bonfires in Hertfordshire which were lit to pray for the departed.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> The rising popularity of <a href="/wiki/Guy_Fawkes_Night" title="Guy Fawkes Night">Guy Fawkes Night</a> (5 November) from 1605 onward, saw many Halloween traditions appropriated by that holiday instead, and Halloween's popularity waned in Britain, with the noteworthy exception of Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-rogers_kirk_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rogers_kirk-117">[117]</a></sup> There and in Ireland, they had been celebrating Samhain and Halloween since at least the early <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>, and the Scottish <a href="/wiki/Kirk" title="Kirk">kirk</a> took a more pragmatic approach to Halloween, seeing it as important to the life cycle and <a href="/wiki/Rites_of_passage" class="mw-redirect" title="Rites of passage">rites of passage</a> of communities and thus ensuring its survival in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-rogers_kirk_117-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rogers_kirk-117">[117]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In France, some Christian families, on the night of All Hallows' Eve, <a href="/wiki/Christian_prayer" title="Christian prayer">prayed</a> beside the graves of their loved ones, setting down dishes full of milk for them.<sup id="cite_ref-Think_page_15_105-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Think_page_15-105">[105]</a></sup> On Halloween, in Italy, some families left a large meal out for <a href="/wiki/Ghost#Judæo-Christian" title="Ghost">ghosts</a> of their passed relatives, before they departed for <a href="/wiki/Church_service" title="Church service">church services</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> In Spain, on this night, special pastries are baked, known as "bones of the holy" (Spanish: <i lang="es">Huesos de Santo</i>) and put them on the graves of the <a href="/wiki/Churchyard" title="Churchyard">churchyard</a>, a practice that continues to this day.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Halloween_Encyclopedia_page_9_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Halloween_Encyclopedia_page_9-119">[119]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spread_to_North_America">Spread to North America</span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Greenwich_Village_Halloween_Parade_(6451249051).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Greenwich_Village_Halloween_Parade_%286451249051%29.jpg/250px-Greenwich_Village_Halloween_Parade_%286451249051%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="166" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Greenwich_Village_Halloween_Parade_%286451249051%29.jpg/375px-Greenwich_Village_Halloween_Parade_%286451249051%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Greenwich_Village_Halloween_Parade_%286451249051%29.jpg/500px-Greenwich_Village_Halloween_Parade_%286451249051%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1063"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Greenwich_Village_Halloween_Parade_(6451249051).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The annual <a href="/wiki/Greenwich_Village_Halloween_Parade" class="mw-redirect" title="Greenwich Village Halloween Parade">Greenwich Village Halloween Parade</a> in <a href="/wiki/Manhattan" title="Manhattan">Manhattan</a> is the world's largest Halloween parade.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/Lesley_Bannatyne" title="Lesley Bannatyne">Lesley Bannatyne</a> and Cindy Ott write that <a href="/wiki/Anglican" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglican">Anglican</a> colonists in the southern United States and <a href="/wiki/Catholic" class="mw-redirect" title="Catholic">Catholic</a> colonists in <a href="/wiki/Maryland" title="Maryland">Maryland</a> "recognized All Hallow's Eve in their church calendars",<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> although the Puritans of <a href="/wiki/New_England" title="New England">New England</a> maintained strong opposition to the holiday, along with other traditional celebrations of the established Church, including Christmas.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Almanac" title="Almanac">Almanacs</a> of the late 18th and early 19th century give no indication that Halloween was widely celebrated in North America.<sup id="cite_ref-rogers_nw_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rogers_nw-123">[123]</a></sup> It was not until mass Irish and Scottish immigration in the 19th century that Halloween became a major holiday in America,<sup id="cite_ref-rogers_nw_123-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rogers_nw-123">[123]</a></sup> confined to the immigrant communities during the mid-19th century. It was gradually assimilated into mainstream society and was celebrated coast to coast by people of all social, racial, and religious backgrounds by the first decade of the 20th century.<sup id="cite_ref-rogers_nationwide_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rogers_nationwide-124">[124]</a></sup> "In <a href="/wiki/Cajun" class="mw-redirect" title="Cajun">Cajun</a> areas, a nocturnal Mass was said in cemeteries on Halloween night. Candles that had been blessed were placed on graves, and families sometimes spent the entire night at the graveside".<sup id="cite_ref-Morton2003_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Morton2003-125">[125]</a></sup> The yearly <a href="/wiki/Greenwich_Village_Halloween_Parade" class="mw-redirect" title="Greenwich Village Halloween Parade">Greenwich Village Halloween Parade</a> was begun in 1974 by puppeteer and mask maker Ralph Lee of <a href="/wiki/Greenwich_Village" title="Greenwich Village">Greenwich Village</a>; it is the world's largest Halloween parade and America's only major nighttime parade, attracting more than 60,000 costumed participants, two million spectators, and a worldwide television audience.<sup id="cite_ref-NYCHalloweenParade_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYCHalloweenParade-126">[126]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Symbols">Symbols</span></h2> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Halloween_Witch_2011.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Halloween_Witch_2011.JPG/260px-Halloween_Witch_2011.JPG" decoding="async" width="260" height="195" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Halloween_Witch_2011.JPG/390px-Halloween_Witch_2011.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Halloween_Witch_2011.JPG/520px-Halloween_Witch_2011.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2288" data-file-height="1712"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Halloween_Witch_2011.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>At Halloween, yards, public spaces, and some houses may be decorated with traditionally macabre symbols including <a href="/wiki/Witch" class="mw-redirect" title="Witch">witches</a>, <a href="/wiki/Skeleton" title="Skeleton">skeletons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ghost" title="Ghost">ghosts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cobwebs" class="mw-redirect" title="Cobwebs">cobwebs</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Headstone" title="Headstone">headstones</a>.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Development of <a href="/wiki/Artefact_(archaeology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Artefact (archaeology)">artifacts</a> and <a href="/wiki/Symbol" title="Symbol">symbols</a> associated with Halloween formed over time. <a href="/wiki/Jack-o%27-lantern" title="Jack-o'-lantern">Jack-o'-lanterns</a> are traditionally carried by <a href="/wiki/Guising" class="mw-redirect" title="Guising">guisers</a> on All Hallows' Eve in order to frighten <a href="/wiki/Unclean_spirit" title="Unclean spirit">evil spirits</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-rogers57_102-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rogers57-102">[102]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> There is a popular <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_Ireland" title="Christianity in Ireland">Irish Christian</a> folktale associated with the jack-o'-lantern,<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> which in <a href="/wiki/Folklore" title="Folklore">folklore</a> is said to represent a "<a href="/wiki/Soul_in_the_Bible" title="Soul in the Bible">soul</a> who has been denied entry into both <a href="/wiki/Heaven_(Christianity)" class="mw-redirect" title="Heaven (Christianity)">heaven</a> and <a href="/wiki/Christian_views_on_Hell" title="Christian views on Hell">hell</a>":<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> </p> 
      <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style>
      <blockquote class="templatequote">
       <p>On route home after a night's drinking, Jack encounters the <a href="/wiki/Devil_in_Christianity" title="Devil in Christianity">Devil</a> and tricks him into climbing a tree. A quick-thinking Jack etches the <a href="/wiki/Sign_of_the_cross" title="Sign of the cross">sign of the cross</a> into the bark, thus trapping the Devil. Jack strikes a bargain that <a href="/wiki/Satan" title="Satan">Satan</a> can never claim his soul. After a life of <a href="/wiki/Christian_views_on_sin" title="Christian views on sin">sin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christian_views_on_alcohol" title="Christian views on alcohol">drink</a>, and mendacity, Jack is refused entry to heaven when he dies. Keeping his promise, the Devil refuses to let Jack into hell and throws a live coal straight from the fires of hell at him. It was a cold night, so Jack places the coal in a hollowed out turnip to stop it from going out, since which time Jack and his lantern have been roaming looking for a place to rest.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup></p>
      </blockquote>
      <p> In Ireland and Scotland, the <a href="/wiki/Turnip" title="Turnip">turnip</a> has traditionally been carved during Halloween,<sup id="cite_ref-lant_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lant-131">[131]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-frle_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-frle-132">[132]</a></sup> but immigrants to North America used the native pumpkin, which is both much softer and much larger&nbsp;– making it easier to carve than a turnip.<sup id="cite_ref-lant_131-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lant-131">[131]</a></sup> The American tradition of carving pumpkins is recorded in 1837<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> and was originally associated with harvest time in general, not becoming specifically associated with Halloween until the mid-to-late 19th century.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Weatherly_PA_Halloween_house.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Weatherly_PA_Halloween_house.jpg/260px-Weatherly_PA_Halloween_house.jpg" decoding="async" width="260" height="195" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Weatherly_PA_Halloween_house.jpg/390px-Weatherly_PA_Halloween_house.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Weatherly_PA_Halloween_house.jpg/520px-Weatherly_PA_Halloween_house.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4270" data-file-height="3202"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Weatherly_PA_Halloween_house.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Decorated house in <a href="/wiki/Weatherly,_Pennsylvania" title="Weatherly, Pennsylvania">Weatherly, Pennsylvania</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The modern imagery of Halloween comes from many sources, including <a href="/wiki/Christian_eschatology" title="Christian eschatology">Christian eschatology</a>, national customs, works of <a href="/wiki/Gothic_fiction" title="Gothic fiction">Gothic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Horror_fiction" title="Horror fiction">horror</a> literature (such as the novels <i><a href="/wiki/Frankenstein" title="Frankenstein">Frankenstein</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Dracula" title="Dracula">Dracula</a></i>) and classic horror films (such as <i><a href="/wiki/Frankenstein_(1931_film)" title="Frankenstein (1931 film)">Frankenstein</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Mummy_(1932_film)" title="The Mummy (1932 film)">The Mummy</a></i>).<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-rogers_h_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rogers_h-136">[136]</a></sup> Imagery of the <a href="/wiki/Skull_and_crossbones_(Spanish_cemetery)" title="Skull and crossbones (Spanish cemetery)">skull</a>, a reference to <a href="/wiki/Golgotha" class="mw-redirect" title="Golgotha">Golgotha</a> in the Christian tradition, serves as "a reminder of death and the transitory quality of human life" and is consequently found in <i><a href="/wiki/Memento_mori" title="Memento mori">memento mori</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Vanitas" title="Vanitas">vanitas</a></i> compositions;<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> skulls have therefore been commonplace in Halloween, which touches on this theme.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> Traditionally, the back walls of churches are "decorated with a depiction of the <a href="/wiki/Last_Judgment" title="Last Judgment">Last Judgment</a>, complete with graves opening and the dead rising, with a heaven filled with angels and a hell filled with devils", a motif that has permeated the observance of this <a href="/wiki/Allhallowtide" title="Allhallowtide">triduum</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> One of the earliest works on the subject of Halloween is from Scottish poet <a href="/wiki/John_Mayne" title="John Mayne">John Mayne</a>, who, in 1780, made note of <a href="/wiki/Prank" class="mw-redirect" title="Prank">pranks</a> at Halloween; <i>"What fearfu' pranks ensue!"</i>, as well as the supernatural associated with the night, <i>"Bogies"</i> (ghosts), influencing <a href="/wiki/Robert_Burns" title="Robert Burns">Robert Burns</a>' "<a href="/wiki/Halloween_(poem)" title="Halloween (poem)">Halloween</a>" (1785).<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> Elements of the <a href="/wiki/Autumn" title="Autumn">autumn season</a>, such as pumpkins, corn <a href="/wiki/Husk" title="Husk">husks</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Scarecrow" title="Scarecrow">scarecrows</a>, are also prevalent. Homes are often decorated with these types of symbols around Halloween. Halloween imagery includes themes of death, <a href="/wiki/Evil" title="Evil">evil</a>, and mythical <a href="/wiki/Monster" title="Monster">monsters</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Black_cat" title="Black cat">Black cats</a>, which have been long associated with witches, are also a common symbol of Halloween. Black, orange, and sometimes purple are Halloween's traditional colors.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div style="clear:both;"></div> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Trick-or-treating_and_guising">Trick-or-treating and guising</span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Trick-or-treating" title="Trick-or-treating">Trick-or-treating</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Trick_or_treat_in_sweden.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Trick_or_treat_in_sweden.jpeg/170px-Trick_or_treat_in_sweden.jpeg" decoding="async" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Trick_or_treat_in_sweden.jpeg/255px-Trick_or_treat_in_sweden.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Trick_or_treat_in_sweden.jpeg/340px-Trick_or_treat_in_sweden.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="1920" data-file-height="2560"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Trick_or_treat_in_sweden.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Trick-or-treaters in <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Trick-or-treating is a customary celebration for children on Halloween. Children go in costume from house to house, asking for treats such as <a href="/wiki/Confectionary" class="mw-redirect" title="Confectionary">candy</a> or sometimes money, with the question, "Trick or treat?" The word "trick" implies a "threat" to perform mischief on the homeowners or their property if no treat is given.<sup id="cite_ref-Britannica_89-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-89">[89]</a></sup> The practice is said to have roots in the medieval practice of <a href="/wiki/Mumming" class="mw-redirect" title="Mumming">mumming</a>, which is closely related to <a href="/wiki/Souling" class="mw-redirect" title="Souling">souling</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> John Pymm wrote that "many of the feast days associated with the presentation of mumming plays were celebrated by the Christian Church."<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> These <a href="/wiki/Feast_day" class="mw-redirect" title="Feast day">feast days</a> included All Hallows' Eve, Christmas, <a href="/wiki/Twelfth_Night_(holiday)" title="Twelfth Night (holiday)">Twelfth Night</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shrove_Tuesday" title="Shrove Tuesday">Shrove Tuesday</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup> Mumming practiced in Germany, Scandinavia and other parts of Europe,<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> involved masked persons in <a href="/wiki/Fancy_dress" class="mw-redirect" title="Fancy dress">fancy dress</a> who "paraded the streets and entered houses to dance or play dice in silence".<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Waterdown_Public_School,_Ontario,_1928_halloween_costume.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Waterdown_Public_School%2C_Ontario%2C_1928_halloween_costume.jpg/180px-Waterdown_Public_School%2C_Ontario%2C_1928_halloween_costume.jpg" decoding="async" width="180" height="278" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Waterdown_Public_School%2C_Ontario%2C_1928_halloween_costume.jpg/270px-Waterdown_Public_School%2C_Ontario%2C_1928_halloween_costume.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Waterdown_Public_School%2C_Ontario%2C_1928_halloween_costume.jpg/360px-Waterdown_Public_School%2C_Ontario%2C_1928_halloween_costume.jpg 2x" data-file-width="582" data-file-height="900"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Waterdown_Public_School,_Ontario,_1928_halloween_costume.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Girl in a Halloween costume in 1928, <a href="/wiki/Ontario" title="Ontario">Ontario</a>, Canada, the same province where the Scottish Halloween custom of <a href="/wiki/Guising" class="mw-redirect" title="Guising">guising</a> is first recorded in North America
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In England, from the medieval period,<sup id="cite_ref-Carmichael2012_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carmichael2012-149">[149]</a></sup> up until the 1930s,<sup id="cite_ref-Hood2014_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hood2014-150">[150]</a></sup> people practiced the Christian custom of souling on Halloween, which involved groups of soulers, both Protestant and Catholic,<sup id="cite_ref-Mosteller_113-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mosteller-113">[113]</a></sup> going from <a href="/wiki/Parish_(Church_of_England)" title="Parish (Church of England)">parish</a> to parish, begging the rich for soul cakes, in exchange for <a href="/wiki/Christian_prayer" title="Christian prayer">praying</a> for the souls of the givers and their friends.<sup id="cite_ref-Dodge_91-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dodge-91">[91]</a></sup> In the Philippines, the practice of souling is called <a href="/wiki/Pangangaluwa" class="mw-redirect" title="Pangangaluwa">Pangangaluwa</a> and is practiced on All Hallow's Eve among children in rural areas.<sup id="cite_ref-Fieldhouse2017p256_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fieldhouse2017p256-21">[21]</a></sup> People drape themselves in white cloths to represent souls and then visit houses, where they sing in return for prayers and sweets.<sup id="cite_ref-Fieldhouse2017p256_21-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fieldhouse2017p256-21">[21]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In Scotland and Ireland, <a href="/wiki/Guising" class="mw-redirect" title="Guising">guising</a> – children disguised in costume going from door to door for food or coins – is a traditional Halloween custom.<sup id="cite_ref-Irish_Times_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Irish_Times-151">[151]</a></sup> It is recorded in Scotland at Halloween in 1895 where masqueraders in disguise carrying lanterns made out of scooped out turnips, visit homes to be rewarded with cakes, fruit, and money.<sup id="cite_ref-frle_132-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-frle-132">[132]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Definition_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Definition-152">[152]</a></sup> In Ireland, the most popular phrase for kids to shout (until the 2000s) was "<a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/help_the_Halloween_party" class="extiw" title="wiktionary:help the Halloween party">Help the Halloween Party</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-Irish_Times_151-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Irish_Times-151">[151]</a></sup> The practice of guising at Halloween in North America is first recorded in 1911, where a newspaper in <a href="/wiki/Kingston,_Ontario" title="Kingston, Ontario">Kingston, Ontario</a>, Canada reported children going "guising" around the neighborhood.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>American historian and author <a href="/wiki/Ruth_Edna_Kelley" title="Ruth Edna Kelley">Ruth Edna Kelley</a> of <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts" title="Massachusetts">Massachusetts</a> wrote the first book-length history of Halloween in the US; <i><a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/The_Book_of_Hallowe%27en" class="extiw" title="s:The Book of Hallowe'en">The Book of Hallowe'en</a></i> (1919), and references souling in the chapter "Hallowe'en in America".<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup> In her book, Kelley touches on customs that arrived from across the Atlantic; "Americans have fostered them, and are making this an occasion something like what it must have been in its best days overseas. All Halloween customs in the United States are borrowed directly or adapted from those of other countries".<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>While the first reference to "guising" in North America occurs in 1911, another reference to ritual begging on Halloween appears, place unknown, in 1915, with a third reference in Chicago in 1920.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup> The earliest known use in print of the term "trick or treat" appears in 1927, in the <i>Blackie Herald</i>, of <a href="/wiki/Blackie,_Alberta" title="Blackie, Alberta">Alberta</a>, Canada.<sup id="cite_ref-Canada_1927_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canada_1927-157">[157]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Halloween_Trunk-or-Treat.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Halloween_Trunk-or-Treat.jpg/220px-Halloween_Trunk-or-Treat.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Halloween_Trunk-or-Treat.jpg/330px-Halloween_Trunk-or-Treat.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Halloween_Trunk-or-Treat.jpg/440px-Halloween_Trunk-or-Treat.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="450"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Halloween_Trunk-or-Treat.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>An <a href="/wiki/Trunk_(automobile)" class="mw-redirect" title="Trunk (automobile)">automobile trunk</a> at a trunk-or-treat event at St. John Lutheran Church and Early Learning Center in <a href="/wiki/Darien,_Illinois" title="Darien, Illinois">Darien, Illinois</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The thousands of <a href="/wiki/Halloween_card" title="Halloween card">Halloween postcards</a> produced between the turn of the 20th century and the 1920s commonly show children but not trick-or-treating.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup> Trick-or-treating does not seem to have become a widespread practice in North America until the 1930s, with the first US appearances of the term in 1934,<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup> and the first use in a national publication occurring in 1939.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>A popular variant of trick-or-treating, known as trunk-or-treating (or Halloween tailgating), occurs when "children are offered treats from the trunks of cars parked in a church parking lot", or sometimes, a school parking lot.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Halloween_Encyclopedia_page_9_119-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Halloween_Encyclopedia_page_9-119">[119]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup> In a trunk-or-treat event, the <a href="/wiki/Trunk_(automobile)" class="mw-redirect" title="Trunk (automobile)">trunk</a> (boot) of each automobile is decorated with a certain theme,<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup> such as those of children's literature, movies, <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">scripture</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Job_(role)" class="mw-redirect" title="Job (role)">job roles</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup> Trunk-or-treating has grown in popularity due to its perception as being more safe than going door to door, a point that resonates well with parents, as well as the fact that it "solves the rural conundrum in which homes [are] built a half-mile apart".<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Costumes">Costumes</span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Halloween_costume" title="Halloween costume">Halloween costume</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Halloween costumes are traditionally modeled after supernatural figures such as <a href="/wiki/Vampires" class="mw-redirect" title="Vampires">vampires</a>, monsters, <a href="/wiki/Ghosts" class="mw-redirect" title="Ghosts">ghosts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Skeletons" class="mw-redirect" title="Skeletons">skeletons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Witches" class="mw-redirect" title="Witches">witches</a>, and devils.<sup id="cite_ref-Britannica_89-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-89">[89]</a></sup> Over time, the costume selection extended to include popular characters from fiction, celebrities, and generic <a href="/wiki/Archetypes" class="mw-redirect" title="Archetypes">archetypes</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Ninja" title="Ninja">ninjas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Princess" title="Princess">princesses</a>. </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Halloween_Shop,_Derry,_September_2010_(02).JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Halloween_Shop%2C_Derry%2C_September_2010_%2802%29.JPG/170px-Halloween_Shop%2C_Derry%2C_September_2010_%2802%29.JPG" decoding="async" width="170" height="255" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Halloween_Shop%2C_Derry%2C_September_2010_%2802%29.JPG/255px-Halloween_Shop%2C_Derry%2C_September_2010_%2802%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Halloween_Shop%2C_Derry%2C_September_2010_%2802%29.JPG/340px-Halloween_Shop%2C_Derry%2C_September_2010_%2802%29.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2848" data-file-height="4272"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Halloween_Shop,_Derry,_September_2010_(02).JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Halloween shop in <a href="/wiki/Derry" title="Derry">Derry</a>, Northern Ireland, selling masks
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
